[2025-11-09T05:22:53Z] మాन$: ⚔️ QUIZ FORMAT: “Guru–Sishya Challenge” ↵ 👥 Team Structure ↵ Each team: 3 members — 1 Guru + 2 Sishyas ↵ Roles: ↵ Guru: Mentor who can give limited hints. ↵ Sishya 1 & 2: Active players who take turns answering. ↵ --- ↵ 🎮 Gameplay ↵ 1️⃣ Rounds ↵ Total: 50 questions divided into multiple rounds (e.g., 5 rounds of 10 questions each) ↵ Each round can have a theme or difficulty level (General Knowledge, History, Science, Audio-Visual, Rapid Fire, etc.) ↵ --- ↵ 2️⃣ Tag Team Rules ↵ Start: Sishya 1 begins answering. ↵ Tag Rule: ↵ If Sishya 1 is stumped on a question, they can tag Sishya 2. ↵ Once tagged, Sishya 2 must stay for at least 5 questions before tagging back. ↵ Tagging is strategic — only allowed between questions, not mid-question. ↵ The Guru cannot answer but can advise when to tag or use a hint. ↵ --- ↵ 3️⃣ Guru’s Hint Power ↵ The Guru can give one hint for every 5 questions. ↵ Example: For Q1–5 → 1 hint total ↵ Hints can be used at any question within that block. ↵ Once a hint is used in that block, the next 5 questions cannot receive another. ↵ The hint must be concise and cryptic — no direct answers. ↵ --- ↵ 4️⃣ Question Format ↵ All questions are multiple choice (A, B, C, D). ↵ Each correct answer = 10 points ↵ Wrong answer = 0 points (no negatives, unless you want a competitive twist like –5) ↵ --- ↵ 5️⃣ Head-On Challenge Round (Finale) ↵ Top 2 teams enter a Head-On Duel. ↵ Both teams face the same set of 10 tough questions. ↵ Buzz-in style: First to buzz answers. ↵ Wrong buzz = –10 penalty; Correct = +20. ↵ Guru cannot help in this round — Sishyas only! ↵ --- ↵ 🏆 Scoring Example ↵ Round	Total Qs	Points per Q	Hints	Max Points ↵ Round 1	10	10	2	100 ↵ Round 2	10	10	2	100 ↵ Round 3	10	10	2	100 ↵ Round 4	10	10	2	100 ↵ Round 5 (Head-On)	10	20	0	200 ↵ Total possible: 600 points ↵ --- ↵ ⚙️ Optional Twists ↵ Guru’s Gamble: Guru can “sacrifice” one future hint to double points for the current question. ↵ Silent Round: One round where Guru cannot speak or signal — full test for Sishyas. ↵ Tag Lock: If a team tags more than 3 times in a round, they lose their next hint opportunity. ↵ --- ‎<This message was edited>

[2025-11-09T05:44:19Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: The naval term X finds its ultimate origin in the Greek word ἄφρακτος ναῦς meaning “undefended ship.” It first became popular in the Netherlands during the Eighty Years’ War that ended in 1648. X was a ship that is powered entirely by sail, and prioritizes maneuverability at the expense of size and armament strength. By the end of this war, the Dutch navy had replaced all its heavy ships with the lighter Xs. At this time, the term X meant any sailing warship carrying fewer than 40 guns. The term dropped out of use in the second half of the 19th century, and made a comeback in WW2 to describe an escort ship of intermediate size. ID X.
[2025-11-09T05:44:47Z] Khandoba: sitting duck?

[2025-11-09T05:55:06Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: The term X for a warship smaller than a frigate comes to us from French. The French borrowed it from the Dutch word “corf” meaning a type of basket, in turn borrowed from the Latin “corbis.” This term is associated is with speed, thanks to the small size of these warships. Early Xs had 20 guns or so on a single deck. ID X.

[2025-11-09T06:03:47Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: The emergence and development of the X was related to the invention of the self-propelled Y in the 1860s. A navy now had the potential to destroy a superior enemy battle fleet using steam launches to fire Ys. Cheap, fast boats armed with Ys, called Y boats, were built and became a threat to large capital ships near enemy coasts. In response, a new type of ship was developed that could take out these Y boats. Initially these ships used to be called “Y-boat Xs.” By WW1, all navies just shortened the name to Xs.

[2025-11-09T06:09:39Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: HMS X was a Royal Navy battleship, the design of which revolutionised naval power. The ship's entry into service in 1906 represented such an advance in naval technology that her name came to be associated with an entire generation of battleships, the Xs, as well as the class of ships named after her. Likewise, the generation of ships she made obsolete became known as pre-Xs. Admiral Sir John "Jacky" Fisher, First Sea Lord of the Board of Admiralty, is credited as the father of X. Shortly after he assumed office in 1904, he ordered design studies for a battleship armed solely with 12 in (305 mm) guns and a speed of 21 knots (39 km/h; 24 mph). ID X.

[2025-11-09T07:12:18Z] Ryan Colaço: Great questions, Pavan ↵ A topic I have no clue about... So lots of lurn today ↵ But reminded me of something interesting, within the same martial theme : ↵ During bombing runs over Europe in World War, Allied pilots reported that the sky around them "burst like black popcorn" filled with explosions from German guns. The German term for those guns was long and technical roughly "air-defense cannon" but the pilots began shortening it to a punchy syllable that captured both the noise and the danger. Within a few years, the same word came to describe another kind of assault not with metal, but with words. What term, still used today for harsh criticism, began as a abbreviation for anti-aircraft fire?

[2025-11-09T07:16:31Z] Ryan Colaço: The term flak comes from the German Flugabwehrkanone, meaning “aircraft defense cannon,” used during World War II to describe German anti-aircraft fire aimed at Allied bombers. English-speaking pilots adopted and shortened the word to refer to the bursting shells that filled the sky around them. By the late 1940s, flak had entered everyday English as a metaphor for harsh criticism — a way of describing the feeling of being “under fire” without a single shot being fired.

[2025-11-10T06:36:19Z] Sidhesh K K: The word "tarpaulin" (and its Hindi/Urdu equivalent "tadpatri") comes from the combination of "tar" and "pall", which means a heavy cloth. The term likely originated in the 17th century to describe canvas or a similar heavy cloth that was treated with tar to make it waterproof, especially for covering cargo on ships. "Tadpatri" is a loanword from Persian, with "tad" (covered) and "patri" (cloth) combining to mean a tarpaulin or waterproof cover.

[2025-11-10T13:40:50Z] Ryan Colaço: Around the early 19th century, the world was hurtling toward modernity — science and technology progressing faster than ever before. ↵ Much like today (with AI), this sparked deep public anxiety about what humans could or should do with science. ↵ *A* became a name often invoked in these debates, thanks to their experiments that made dead frog legs twitch with electric shocks... ↵ So often did they appear in popular discourse, in fact, that the name still lives in English: to “A-ize” someone means to spur them into action — and “*B* , which refers to an electrochemical process that protects metals from corrosion, derived from A’s surname. ↵ Many great thinkers joined the conversation on “science gone too far,” including B’s contemporary William Godwin, and the brilliant chemist *C*. Listing C's portfolio would make this Q too easy...but he was a brilliant chemist and inventor, who often used his friends -like poet Samuel Taylor Coleridge and inventor James Watt - as enthusiastic subjects for some of his experiments. Particularly, his work on the euphoric effects of laughing gas made him a very popular friend ;) ↵ Godwin’s child, *D* , would later find themselves stranded with Lord Byron and others at a lakeside villa in Geneva — competing to write the best *E* story (genre). ↵ The resulting story, *F* (from 1818), wasn't just the winner in that small stranded crew...but also remains one of the most famous works of E in English literature. ↵ Its success was immediate — spawning many plays, sensational retellings, and countless adaptations: This was perhaps a double-edged sword...because that monstrous success led to Hollywood incarnations that have notably obscured the original's nuance and humanity. It spawned interpretations and parodies. The very first of which is tied with the very origins of movies: in *G* 's horrifying 1910 short film. ↵ And since then, the story featured or was retold through Hollywood's Universal Pictures and The Rocky Horror Picture Show – and it foreshadowed others, such as 2001: A Space Odyssey. There are Italian and Japanese E-movies...and even a Blaxploitation film. ↵ Most recently, filmmaker *H* reimagined it, thanking D for the inspiration. His movies, like this latest one, often feature a more complex, humanized scary-antagonists...Which he says he learnt from D's work, who he says: ↵ “Picked up the plight of *I* , gave weight to the burden of Prometheus, and gave voice to the voiceless.” ↵ *I* being a similarly resentful, subjugated island creature from Shakespeare's _The Tempest_ , often seen as a symbol of the exploited or misunderstood — and an inspiration for D’s own creation.

[2025-11-10T21:54:12Z] Khandoba: Aaah
[2025-11-10T21:54:31Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: Basically the borough of Manhattan uses New York, NY. All other four boroughs use a non-standard addressing scheme, with the name of the borough rather than the name of the city. So Brooklyn addresses are finished off with Brooklyn, NY; Queens addresses with Queens, NY. This is for two reasons: one, the same street names occur in multiple boroughs (e.g. there is a Broadway in Brooklyn which is different from the more famous Broadway.) Two, the address scheme dates back to the time when today's New York was not one city. When the city got consolidated in 1898, people continued to use addresses like Brooklyn, NY.
